Rulings on Sajdah al-Tilawah
Procedure, evidence, and conditions for Sajdah al-Tilawah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the Hanafi procedure for Sajdah al-Tilawah and cite the athar evidence that supports the ruling.

Core Idea
Sajdah al-Tilawah: only takbir, no tashahhud or taslim, and requires wudu.

This passage from a Hanafi fiqh text outlines the precise procedure for the prostration of recitation (Sajdah al-Tilawah). It specifies the actions, clarifies what is omitted, and provides evidence from the practice of the Companions and successors. Understanding this ruling ensures that one performs this act of worship correctly, avoiding common mistakes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is Sajdah al-Tilawah, a prostration performed upon reciting or hearing a verse of prostration. In the Hanafi school, it is wajib (obligatory).
Obligation is based on the command in the Quran and Sunnah, but this passage assumes the ruling.
2
Extract the procedure
The text states: say takbir without raising hands, prostrate, then say takbir again and raise your head. No tashahhud or taslim follows.
The takbir is said both before and after the prostration.
3
Locate the dalil
The author cites athar (traditions) from Abdullah ibn Mas'ud's father, Sa'id ibn Jubayr, and Al-Hasan, which show the practice without tashahhud or taslim.
These are recorded in Musannaf Ibn Abi Shayba.
4
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
The athar demonstrate that the Prophet's companions and successors performed the prostration simply with takbir, without additional prayer elements. This establishes the procedure as the sunnah.
The absence of tashahhud and taslim is deduced from the fact that they are not reported.
5
Note additional conditions
The passage also mentions that wudu is necessary because Sajdah al-Tilawah is part of prayer, citing an athar from Ibrahim al-Nakha'i. Also, if the verse is repeated before the obligation is fulfilled, tadakhul (merger) applies, requiring only one prostration.
Tadakhul is a Hanafi rule: repeated causes merge into one obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصلوة
۳۲۷ (۹) ومن اراد السجود كبر ولا يرفع يديه وسجد ثم كبر ورفع رأسه ولا تشهد عليه ولا سلام.
میں کئی مرتبہ آیت سجدہ پڑھا تو تداخل ہوگا اور ایک ہی سجدہ لازم ہوگا۔ [۳۲۷] (۹) جس نے سجدہ تلاوت کا ارادہ کیا تو تکبیر کہے اور ہاتھ نہ اٹھائے اور سجدہ کرے، پھر تکبیر کہے اور اپنے سرکو اٹھائے۔ اس پر تشہد نہ پڑھے اور نہ سلام کرے۔
اثر میں ہے عن عبد الله بن مسلم قال كان ابي اذا قرأ السجدة قال الله اكبر ثم سجد (الف) (مصنف ابن ابی شیبة ۲۰۲، باب من قال اذا قرأت السجدة فكبر وسجد ج اول ص ۱۳۶۴ نمبر ۴۱۸۷) اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ صرف تکبیر کہہ کر سجدہ میں جائے گا۔ تشہد نہیں پڑھے گا اس کے لئے یہ اثر دلیل ہے عن سعيد بن جبيـر انـه كـان يـقـرأ السجدة فيرفع رأسه ولا يسلم، قال كان الحسن يقرأ بنا سجود القرآن ولا يسلم (ب) (مصنف ابن ابی شیبۃ ۲۰۱ ، باب من کان لا يسلم من السجدة ج اول ص ۳۶۴ نمبر ۴۱۸۳/۴۱۸۴) اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ سجدہ تلاوت میں تشہد اور سلام نہیں ہیں۔ صرف تکبیر کہہ کر سجدہ کرے پھر تکبیر کہہ کر سر اٹھائے بس اتنا ہی کافی ہے۔ سجدہ تلاوت نماز کا حصہ ہے اس لئے اس کے لئے وضو ضروری ہے۔ اس کے لئے اثر ہے عـن ابــراهيــم قـال اذا سمعه وهو على غير وضوء فليتوضأ ثم ليقرأ فليسجد (ج) (مصنف ابن ابى هيبة ۲۲۰، باب في الرجل يسجد السجدة وهو على غير وضوء ج اول ص (۴۶۷
حاشیہ : (الف) عبد اللہ بن مسلم کہتے ہیں کہ میرے والد جب آیت سجدہ پڑھتے تو کہتے اللہ اکبر پھر سجدہ کرتے (ب) سعید بن جبیر آیت سجدہ پڑھتے پھر سر اٹھاتے اور سلام نہیں کرتے حسن ہمیں سجدہ قرآن پڑھاتے اور سلام نہیں کرتے (ج) ابراہیم نے کہا جب آیت سجدہ سنے اور وہ وضو پر نہ ہو تو وضو کرے پھر پڑھے پھر سجدہ کرے۔
The Book of Prayer. 327 (9) Whoever intends to prostrate, he says takbir and does not raise his hands, and prostrates, then says takbir and raises his head. There is no tashahhud upon him, nor taslim. [Urdu:] If he recites a verse of prostration multiple times, then tadakhul will occur and only one prostration becomes necessary. [327] (9) Whoever intends to perform the prostration of recitation, he says takbir and does not raise his hands, and performs prostration, then says takbir and raises his head. He does not recite tashahhud upon it, nor taslim. There is an athar from Abdullah ibn Muslim who said: 'My father, when he recited a prostration verse, would say Allahu Akbar, then prostrate.' (Ibn Abi Shayba, 202). This athar shows that he goes into prostration by merely saying takbir. He will not recite tashahhud. Another athar from Sa'id ibn Jubayr: 'He would recite the prostration verse, then raise his head and not make taslim.' Also al-Hasan used to lead us in the prostrations of the Quran and not make taslim. (Ibn Abi Shayba, 201). These athar show that there is no tashahhud nor taslim in prostration of recitation. Simply say takbir, prostrate, then say takbir, raise the head. That is sufficient. The prostration of recitation is part of the prayer, so wudu is necessary for it. There is an athar from Ibrahim: 'When he hears it while not in a state of wudu, he should perform wudu, then recite, then prostrate.' (Ibn Abi Shayba, 220). Footnote: (a) ... (b) ... (c) ...
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This is a ruling on the method of performing Sajdah al-Tilawah (prostration of recitation). The author begins by stating the procedure: one should say takbir without raising the hands, then prostrate, then say takbir again while raising the head. Importantly, there is no tashahhud (testification of faith) nor taslim (salutation of peace) following this prostration. The Urdu commentary adds a note on tadakhul: if a person recites or hears a verse of prostration multiple times, only one prostration is necessary. The author then provides athar (traditions from companions and successors) as evidence. The first athar from Abdullah ibn Mas'ud shows that his father would simply say 'Allahu Akbar' and prostrate without any additional actions. The second athar from Sa'id ibn Jubayr and al-Hasan confirms that no taslim is made after the prostration. The author then affirms that these athar establish the ruling. Finally, the author states that since Sajdah al-Tilawah is part of prayer, wudu (ablution) is required for it. This is supported by an athar from Ibrahim al-Nakha'i: one must have wudu before reciting and prostrating. Thus, the passage covers the procedure, the necessity of wudu, and the proofs from the pious predecessors.

Key Terms
سجدة التلاوة (sajdat al-tilawah): Prostration performed upon reciting or hearing a specific verse of Quran that contains a command to prostrate.
تداخل (tadakhul): Merger or overlapping of acts; here, multiple recitations of a prostration verse require only one prostration.
تشهد (tashahhud): The testimony of faith recited in the sitting posture of prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the correct procedure for Sajdah al-Tilawah?
A) Say takbir, raise hands, prostrate, then recite tashahhud and taslim.
B) Say takbir without raising hands, prostrate, say takbir again, raise head. No tashahhud or taslim.
C) Perform two prostrations with tashahhud between them.
D) Only recite the verse without any prostration.

Key Takeaway
Sajdah al-Tilawah consists of two takbirs and one prostration, requires wudu, and does not include tashahhud or taslim.

Definition of Travel Distance for Legal Concessions
Defines the travel distance that triggers legal dispensations like shortening prayer and wiping over khuffain

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of travel (safar) that triggers legal concessions and calculate the distance in miles.

Core Idea
Three days' journey (48 miles) on foot or camel pace is the Hanafi threshold for travel concessions.

The chapter on the traveller's prayer begins by establishing what constitutes a journey that changes legal rulings. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi definition of travel, the evidence from hadith, and the calculation of 48 miles. Understanding this threshold is essential for applying concessions such as shortening prayer (qasr) and extended wiping over socks (masah).

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal act and its ruling
The passage focuses on the travel (safar) that triggers concessions like qasr prayer. The Hanafi ruling is that qasr becomes wajib for a journey of three days or more.
qasr is the shortening of four-rak'at prayers to two.
2
Examine the definition of travel
Travel is defined as intending a destination three days away, measured by average camel or walking pace from morning to noon. Faster modes are not considered.
The Shari'ah considers the general state of people, hence the average pace.
3
Analyze the primary dalil
The hadith of Abu Sa'id al-Khudri forbids a woman to travel three days or more without a mahram, establishing three days as the minimum for travel.
This is a hadith ahad, zanni in both transmission and meaning.
4
Calculate the distance
Average walking speed is 16 miles per half-day, so three days equal 48 miles. This is the well-known Hanafi standard.
Some later scholars equate 48 miles to approximately 77 km.
5
Note cross-madhhab differences
The Shafi'i school considers two days' journey as the threshold, though the actual distance may be similar. The Maliki and Hanbali schools also approximate 48 miles but with variations.
These differences affect the practical application of travel rulings.

ضروری نوٹ آدمی سفر میں چلا جائے تو اس کو مسافر کہتے ہیں۔ سفر کی حالت میں آدمی آدھی نماز پڑھے اس کی دلیل یہ حدیث ہے سمع ابن عمر يقول صحبت رسول الله فكان لايزيد في السفر على ركعتین و ابا بكر و عمر و عثمان كذلک (الف) ( بخاری شریف ، باب من لم يتطوع في السفر و بر الصلوات ص ۱۴۹ نمبر ۱۱۰۲ر مسلم شریف ، باب صلوۃ المسافرین وقصر صاص ۲۴۲ نمبر ۶۸۹) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ حضور اور صحابہ نے سفر میں دو رکعت سے زیادہ نماز نہیں پڑھی۔ [۳۲۸] (۱) جس سفر سے احکام بدل جاتے ہیں یہ ہے کہ انسان ایسی جگہ کا ارادہ کرے کہ اس کے درمیان اور اس کے مقصد کے درمیان تین دن کا سفر ہو اونٹ کی چال سے یا قدم کی چال سے اور نہیں اعتبار ہے اس میں پانی میں چلنے کا۔

آدمی عموما ایک دن میں اوسط چال سے صبح سے دو پہر تک میں سولہ (۱۶) میل چل سکتا ہے۔ اس اعتبار سے تین دن میں اڑتالیس (۴۸) میل ہوتے ہیں۔ اور حنفیوں کے یہاں اڑتالیس میل اسی حساب سے مشہور ہے۔ تین دن کے سفر کا اعتبار اس حدیث سے ہے عن ابي سعيد الخدري قال قال رسول الله لا يحل لامرأة تؤمن بالله واليوم الآخر ان تسافر سفرا يكون ثلاثه ايام فصاعدا الا و معها ابوها او ابنها او زوجها او اخوها او ذومحرم منها .
(ب) ( مسلم شریف ، باب سفر المرأة مع محرم الی حج وغیرہ ص ۴۳۴ ابواب الج نمبر ۱۳۴۰ / بخاری شریف ، باب کم اقام النبی فی حجتہ ص ۱۴۷ ، ابواب تقصیر الصلوۃ نمبر ۱۰۸۸) اس حدیث میں جس مسافت کو سفر قرار دیا ہے وہ تین دن کی مسافت ہے۔ اس لئے تین دن کی مسافت پر نماز کے قصر کا حکم لگایا جائے گا (۲) موزے پر سح میں بھی تین دن کے سفر کو سفر قرار دینے کا اشارہ ملتا ہے۔ حدیث یہ ہے قال اتيت عائشة اسألها عن المسح على الخفين ... فقال جعل رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ثلاثة ايام ولياليهن للمسافر ويوما وليلة للمقيم (ج) (مسلم شریف ، باب التوقيت في اسمع علی الخفین ص ۱۳۵ نمبر ۱۷۶ ابوداؤد شریف ، باب التوقیت فی المسح ص ۲۳ نمبر ۱۵۷) اس حدیث سے
حاشیہ : (الف) حضرت ابن عمر فرماتے ہیں کہ میں حضور کے ساتھ رہا وہ سفر میں دو رکعت سے زیادہ نہیں کرتے ۔ اور ابوبکر ، عمر اور حضرت عثمان بھی ایسا ہی کیا کرتے تھے (ب) آپ نے فرمایا کسی عورت کے لئے حلال نہیں جو اللہ اور آخرت کے دن پر ایمان رکھتی ہو کہ وہ سفر کرے ایسا سفر جو تین دن یا اس سے زیادہ کا ہومگر اس کے ساتھ اس کا باپ، یا اس کا بیٹا یا اس کا شوہر یا اس کا بھائی یا اس کا زی محرم ہو (ج) میں حضرت عائشہ کے پاس آیا مسح علی الخفین کے بارے میں پوچھنے کے لئے۔ ر حضرت علی نے فرمایا کہ حضور نے تین دن تین راتیں مسافر کے لئے اور ایک دن اور ایک رات مقیم کے لئے متعین کیا۔
Chapter on the Prayer of the Traveller. [328] (1) The travel by which rulings change is that a person intends a place such that between him and his destination is a journey of three days by the pace of a camel or by the pace of foot, and no consideration is given to travel by water. [Urdu:] Necessary note: When a person goes on a journey, he is called a traveller. In the state of travel, a person prays half the prayer. The proof is the hadith: 'I heard Ibn Umar say: I accompanied the Messenger of Allah and he did not increase beyond two rak'ats in travel, and likewise Abu Bakr, Umar and Uthman.' (Bukhari, Muslim). From this hadith it is understood that the Prophet and the Companions did not pray more than two rak'ats in travel. [328] (1) The travel by which rulings change is that a person intends a place such that between him and his destination is a journey of three days by the pace of a camel or by the pace of foot, and no consideration is given to travel by water. The journey from one place to another should be a three-day route, walking from morning till noon (midday). The pace of a camel and the pace of a man on foot are considered, because man generally walks at this speed. Therefore, the Shari'ah considers this speed. Faster speeds are not considered, because Shari'ah considers the general state of man. A person can usually walk sixteen miles from morning to early afternoon at an average pace. Accordingly, three days amount to forty-eight miles. Among the Hanafis, forty-eight miles is well-known by this calculation. The consideration of three days' journey is based on the hadith of Abu Sa'id al-Khudri: 'The Messenger of Allah said: It is not permissible for a woman who believes in Allah and the Last Day to travel a journey that is three days or more unless with her father, son, husband, brother, or a mahram relative.' (Muslim, Bukhari). In this hadith, the distance defined as travel is a distance of three days. Therefore, the ruling of qasr (shortening) prayer is applied to a distance of three days. (2) Also, in wiping over leather socks, there is an indication that three days' journey is considered travel. The hadith: 'I came to Aisha to ask about wiping over khuffain... She said: The Messenger of Allah set three days and their nights for the traveller, and one day and one night for the resident.' (Muslim, Abu Dawud). From this hadith... [Footnotes]
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on the traveller's prayer (Salah al-Musafir). The author first states a necessary note: a traveller is one who goes on a journey, and he prays half the prayer (i.e., shortens from four to two rak'ats). The proof is the hadith of Ibn Umar that the Prophet and the Caliphs never prayed more than two rak'ats while travelling. Then the author defines the travel that changes legal rulings: it is when a person intends to go to a place that is a three-day journey away, calculated by an average camel's pace or a person's walking pace. This excludes travel by water (i.e., by ship) because that is not measured by days in the same way. The author clarifies that the average walking speed is from morning to noon (roughly half a day), and from this, a person covers sixteen miles per day, thus three days equal forty-eight miles. This is the well-known Hanafi standard. The proof for the three-day criterion is the hadith of Abu Sa'id al-Khudri, which forbids a woman to travel three days or more without a mahram. This establishes three days as the definition of travel. Additionally, the hadith on wiping over leather socks (masah 'ala al-khuffain) differentiates between a traveller (three days) and a resident (one day), further supporting that three days is the measure for travel. Thus, the passage clearly sets the Hanafi position that any journey of three days (approximately 48 miles) triggers the legal concessions such as qasr of prayer and extended masah.

Key Terms
سفر (safar): A journey of three days (48 miles) by average camel or walking pace, triggering legal concessions.
قصر (qasr): Shortening of the four-rak'at obligatory prayers to two rak'ats during travel.
ميل (mil): An Arab mile, approximately 1.6 km; 48 miles = about 77 km.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the minimum travel distance that changes rulings like qasr prayer?
A) One day's journey (16 miles).
B) Two days' journey (32 miles).
C) Three days' journey (48 miles).
D) Four days' journey (64 miles).

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school defines travel for legal concessions as a three-day journey of 48 miles, measured by average walking or camel pace, based on hadith evidence.

Definition of Travel Distance for Qasr (Three Days Journey)
Defines travel distance with proofs and Shafi'i dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of travel distance (three days/48 miles), cite the primary proofs, and explain the practical difference with the Shafi'i school.

Core Idea
Three days' journey by camel or foot is the Hanafi threshold for travel concessions.

This lesson defines the minimum distance that transforms a person from a resident (muqeem) to a traveler (musafir) in the Hanafi school. The ruling affects qasr (shortening prayer), fasting in Ramadan, wiping over khuffs, and other concessions. You will learn the Hanafi position, its proofs from hadith and practice of the Companions, and how it differs from Imam al-Shafi'i's view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi definition of travel
Read the opening statement: 'travel that changes rulings is a journey of three days by camel or foot pace.' Note that water travel is excluded from this definition.
The definition uses the average pace of camel or walking, not faster modes.
2
Locate the primary dalil
The text cites the hadith of Abu Sa'id al-Khudri: 'It is not permissible for a woman to travel three days without a mahram.' This hadith defines the minimum duration labeled as travel.
This is a dalil naqli (textual proof) with zanni thubut and dalalah.
3
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf with Shafi'i
Imam al-Shafi'i uses a hadith prohibiting traveling one day without a mahram. The practical consequence: Hanafis require three days for qasr; Shafi'is require only one day.
The difference is not just theoretical but affects the validity of shortenings.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school gives preference to the three-day hadith because it is more specific and supported by multiple evidences (qasr practice, wiping over khuffs, Companion practice). Caution (ihtiyat) dictates adhering to the longer distance.
This illustrates the Hanafi method of tarjih: preferring the stronger and more consistent dalil.
5
Convert to modern measurements
The text explains that 3 days at average pace = 48 Hanafi miles (1 Hanafi mile = 4000 cubits ~ 2 km). This equals about 55.5 English miles.
Be careful not to confuse with modern miles; the Hanafi mile is longer.

[۳۲۸ (۱) السفر الذي يتغير به الاحكام ان يقصد الانسان موضعا بينه و بينه مسيرة ثلاثة ايام بسير الابل و مشى الاقدام ولا معتبر في ذلك بالسير في الماء.
ضروری نوٹ آدمی سفر میں چلا جائے تو اس کو مسافر کہتے ہیں۔ سفر کی حالت میں آدمی آدھی نماز پڑھے اس کی دلیل یہ حدیث ہے عن ابن عمر يقول صحبت رسول الله فكان لا يزيد في السفر على ركعتين و ابا بكر و عمر و عثمان كذلك (الف) ( بخاری شریف ، باب من لم يتطوع في السفر و بر الصلوات ص ۱۴۹ نمبر ۱۱۰۲ر مسلم شریف ، باب صلوۃ المسافرين و قصر ص ۲۴۲ نمبر ۶۸۹) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ حضور اور صحابہ نے سفر میں دو رکعت سے زیادہ نماز نہیں پڑھی۔
[۳۲۸] (۱) جس سفر سے احکام بدل جاتے ہیں یہ ہے کہ انسان ایسی جگہ کا ارادہ کرے کہ اس کے درمیان اور اس کے مقصد کے درمیان تین دن کا سفر ہو اونٹ کی چال سے یا قدم کی چال سے اور نہیں اعتبار ہے اس میں پانی میں چلنے کا۔
جس مقام سے جس مقام تک جانا ہے وہاں کا سفر تین دن کا راستہ ہو۔ درمیانی چال سے کہ صبح سے زوال تک چلے۔ اور اونٹ کی چال اور انسان کی پیدل چال کا اعتبار ہے۔ کیونکہ انسان عام طور پر اسی رفتار سے چلتا ہے۔ اس لئے شریعت نے اس کی چال کا اعتبار کیا ہے۔ اس سے تیز رفتار کی چال کا اعتبار نہیں کیا۔ کیونکہ شریعت انسان کی عمومی حالت کا اعتبار کرتی ہے۔
آدمی عموما ایک دن میں اوسط چال سے صبح سے دو پہر تک میں سولہ (۱۶) میل چل سکتا ہے۔ اس اعتبار سے تین دن میں اڑتالیس (۴۸) میل ہوتے ہیں۔ اور حنفیوں کے یہاں اڑتالیس میل اسی حساب سے مشہور ہے۔ تین دن کے سفر کا اعتبار اس حدیث سے ہے عن ابي سعيد الخدري قال قال رسول الله لا يحل لامرأة تؤمن بالله واليوم الآخر ان تسافر سفرا يكون ثلاثه ايام فصاعدا الا و معها ابوها او ابنها او زوجها او اخوها او ذو محرم منها (ب) ( مسلم شریف ، باب سفر المرأة مع محرم الی حج وغیرہ ص ۴۳۴ ابواب الحج نمبر ۱۳۴۰ / بخاری شریف ، باب كم اقام النبی فی حجتہ ص ۱۴۷ ، ابواب تقصیر الصلوۃ نمبر ۱۰۸۸) اس حدیث میں جس مسافت کو سفر قرار دیا ہے وہ تین دن کی مسافت ہے۔ اس لئے تین دن کی مسافت پر نماز کے قصر کا حکم لگایا جائے گا (۲) موزے پر مسح میں بھی تین دن کے سفر کو سفر قرار دینے کا اشارہ ملتا ہے۔ حديث ہے قال اتيت عائشة اسألها عن المسح على الخفين ... فقال جعل رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ثلاثة ايام ولياليهن للمسافر ويوما وليلة للمقيم (ج) (مسلم شریف ، باب التوقيت في المسح على الخفين ص ۱۳۵ نمبر ۱۷۶ ابوداؤد شریف ، باب التوقیت فی المسح ص ۲۳ نمبر ۱۵۷) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوتا کہ سفر کی مدت تین دن ہونی چاہئے۔ اسی کو سفر شرعی کہیں گے (۳) اس اثر سے بھی اس کی تائید ہوتی ہے كان ابن عمر و ابن عباس يقصران و يفطران في اربعة برد وهو ستة عشر فرسخا (الف) ( بخاری شریف، باب في كم يقصر الصلوۃ ص ۱۴۷ نمبر ۱۰۸۶) ایک فرسخ تین میل شرعی کا ہوتا ہے اس اعتبار سے سولہ فرسخ اڑتالیس میل ہوئے۔ اور انگریزی میل چھوٹا ہوتا ہے اس لئے وہ ساڑھے چون میل انگریزی ہوئے۔ ایک دن میں وسط چال کے ساتھ عموما سولہ میل سفر طے کر پاتے ہیں۔ اس لئے تین دن میں اڑتالیس میل ہوئے اور اصل تین دن کا سفر ہے۔ میل کو متعین کرنا سہولت کے لئے ہے۔ 
امام شافعی کے نزدیک ایک دن ایک رات کی مسافت ہو تب بھی قصر کر سکتا ہے۔ ان کی دلیل یہ قول ہے عن ابي هريرة قال قال النبي الا لا يحل لامرأة تؤمن بالله واليوم الآخر ان تسافر يوم وليلة ليس معها حرمة (ب) ( بخاری شریف، باب فی کم تقصیر الصلو ص ۳۸ نمبر ۱۰۸۸) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ ایک دن اور ایک رات کے سفر کو بھی سفر کہتے ہیں۔ اس لئے اس پر بھی قصر ہو سکتا ہے۔ امام ابو حنیفہ دلائل کی روشنی میں احتیاط کی طرف گئے ہیں۔
انت مقصد : جانے کی جگہ، قصد کرنے کی جگہ، مسیر : سیر سے مشتق ہے، سفر۔
فرسخ میل اور کیلو میٹر کا حساب ہے
پچھلے زمانے میں عرب میں بردہ فريح اور غلوہ رائج تھے، بعد میں میل شرعی آیا اور ابھی دنیا میں انگریزی میل اور کیلو میٹر کا حساب رائج ہے۔ اس لئے ان کی تفصیل یہ ہے۔
ایک بردچار فرسخ کا ہوتا ہے۔ اور ایک فرسخ تین شرعی میل کا ہوتا۔ اور ایک شرعی میل چار ہزار ہاتھ یعنی دو ہزار گز کا ہوتا ہے۔ اس طرح ایک برد بارہ شرعی میل کا ہوا۔ ایک برد چار فرسخ کا ہوتا ہے اس کا ذکر عبد اللہ بن عباس کے اثر میں گزرا۔ كان ابن عمر و ابن عباس يقصران و يفطران في اربعة برد و هو ستة عشر فرسخا ( بخاری شریف، باب في كم يقصر الصلوۃ ص ۱۴۷، نمبر ۱۰۸۶) اس اثر میں ہے کہ چار برد سولہ فرسخ کا ہوتا تھا۔ یعنی ایک برد چار فرسخ کا ۔ اور چار برد سولہ فرسخ کا ہوا جس پر عبد اللہ بن عمر اور عبد اللہ بن عباس سفر کا حکم لگاتے تھے۔ اور ایک فرسخ تین میل کا ہوتا ہے۔ اور ایک میل شرعی چار ہزار ہاتھ کا۔ جس کا مطلب یہ ہوا کہ دو ہزار گز کا میل ہوا۔ اس کی دلیل در مختار کی یہ عبارت ہے۔ الفرسخ : ثلاثۃ اميال والميل : اربعة آلاف ذراع ( رد المحتار على در المختار، باب صلوۃ المسافر ، ج ثانی ،ص ۷۲۵ ) اس عبارت سے معلوم ہوا کہ فرسخ تین میل کا ہوتا ہے۔ اور ایک میل شرعی دو ہزار گز کا ہوتا ہے۔ اب بارہ فرسخ کو تین میل سے ضرب دیں تو 48 میل شرعی ہوئے ۔
حاشیہ : (الف) حضرت ابن عمر فرماتے ہیں کہ میں حضور کے ساتھ رہا وہ سفر میں دو رکعت سے زیادہ نہیں کرتے ۔ اور ابوبکر ، عمر اور حضرت عثمان بھی ایسا ہی کیا کرتے تھے (ب) آپ نے فرمایا کسی عورت کے لئے حلال نہیں جو اللہ اور آخرت کے دن پر ایمان رکھتی ہو کہ وہ سفر کرے ایسا سفر جو تین دن یا اس سے زیادہ کا ہو مگر اس کے ساتھ اس کا باپ، یا اس کا بیٹا یا اس کا شوہر یا اس کا بھائی یا اس کا ذو محرم ہو (ج) میں حضرت عائشہ کے پاس آیا مسح علی الخفین کے بارے میں پوچھنے کے لئے۔ حضرت علی نے فرمایا کہ حضور نے تین دن تین راتیں مسافر کے لئے اور ایک دن اور ایک رات مقیم کے لئے متعین کیا۔
Book of Prayer. Chapter of the Traveler's Prayer. [328 (1)] The travel by which rulings change is that a person intends a place that is three days' journey away by camel pace and foot pace, and there is no consideration in that of traveling by water. Necessary note: When a person goes on a journey, he is called a traveler. In the state of travel, a person prays half the prayer. The proof for this is the hadith from Ibn Umar: 'I accompanied the Messenger of Allah and he would not increase beyond two rak'ahs in travel, and Abu Bakr, Umar, and Uthman did likewise.' (Bukhari, Muslim) From this hadith, it is known that the Prophet and the Companions did not pray more than two rak'ahs in travel. [328] (1) The travel by which rulings change is that a person intends a place such that between him and his intended destination there is a journey of three days by camel pace or foot pace, and there is no consideration in that of traveling by water. The journey from one place to another should be a distance of three days with an intermediate pace, traveling from morning to noon. Consideration is given to camel pace and human foot pace, because people generally walk at that speed. Therefore, the Shariah considers that pace, not a faster pace. A person can generally travel sixteen (16) miles in a day from morning to noon at an average pace. Thus, three days make forty-eight (48) miles, and among the Hanafis, 48 miles is well-known according to this calculation. The basis for three days' travel is the hadith from Abu Sa'id al-Khudri: The Messenger of Allah said: 'It is not permissible for a woman who believes in Allah and the Last Day to travel a journey of three days or more unless she is accompanied by her father, son, husband, brother, or a mahram.' (Muslim, Bukhari) In this hadith, the distance considered as travel is three days. Therefore, the ruling of shortening prayer (qasr) is applied to a distance of three days. (2) There is also an indication in wiping over socks (khuffayn) that three days' travel is considered travel. The hadith: 'I came to Aisha to ask about wiping over socks... She said: The Messenger of Allah set three days and their nights for the traveler, and one day and one night for the resident.' (Muslim, Abu Dawud) From this hadith, it appears that the duration of travel should be three days, and this is called shar'i travel. (3) This is also supported by the practice of Ibn Umar and Ibn Abbas: 'Ibn Umar and Ibn Abbas would shorten (prayer) and break (fast) during a journey of four barid, which is sixteen farsakh.' (Bukhari) One farsakh is three shar'i miles, so sixteen farsakh equals forty-eight miles. The English mile is shorter, so it is 55.5 English miles. In one day, at a moderate pace, one can generally cover sixteen miles. Therefore, three days make forty-eight miles, and the original is a three-day journey. Specifying miles is for convenience. Imam Shafi'i holds that if the distance is one day and one night, one can also shorten prayer. His proof is the hadith from Abu Hurairah: 'It is not permissible for a woman who believes in Allah and the Last Day to travel a distance of one day and one night without a mahram.' (Bukhari) From this hadith, it is understood that one day and one night is also called travel, so qasr can be applied on that. Imam Abu Hanifa leaned towards caution (ihtiyat) in light of the evidences. The term 'maqsad' means the place of destination; 'masirah' is derived from 'sayr' meaning travel. The calculation of farsakh, mile, and kilometer: In ancient times, Arabs used barid, farsakh, and ghulwah; later the shar'i mile came. Now English miles and kilometers are used. One barid equals four farsakh. One farsakh equals three shar'i miles. One shar'i mile equals four thousand cubits, i.e., two thousand yards. Thus one barid equals twelve shar'i miles. The mention of four barid is in the effect of Ibn Abbas: 'Ibn Umar and Ibn Abbas would shorten and break fast on four barid, which is sixteen farsakh.' (Bukhari) That effect states that four barid is sixteen farsakh, meaning one barid is four farsakh. And one farsakh is three miles. One shar'i mile is four thousand cubits, meaning two thousand yards. The proof is from Radd al-Muhtar: 'Al-farsakh: three miles, and al-mil: four thousand cubits.' From this it is known that farsakh is three miles, and one shar'i mile is two thousand yards. Now multiply twelve farsakh by three miles to get 48 shar'i miles. Footnotes: (a) Ibn Umar said: 'I accompanied the Prophet and he would not pray more than two rak'ahs in travel, and Abu Bakr, Umar, and Uthman did likewise.' (b) The Prophet said: 'It is not permissible for a woman who believes in Allah and the Last Day to travel a journey of three days or more unless she is accompanied by her father, son, husband, brother, or a mahram.' (c) I came to Aisha to ask about wiping over socks... He (Ali) said: 'The Prophet set three days and nights for the traveler and one day and night for the resident.'
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute defining the minimum distance of travel that triggers the concession of qasr (shortening prayer) and other travel-related rulings. The author, following the Hanafi school, begins by stating the definition: travel that changes rulings is a journey of three days by camel or foot pace, not by water. The Urdu commentary then provides the primary proof: the hadith of Ibn Umar showing the Prophet and Companions consistently prayed two rak'ahs in travel. This establishes the principle of qasr. Then the text defines the distance more precisely: a three-day journey at an average pace (morning to noon), equivalent to approximately 48 Hanafi miles. This is supported by multiple hadith: (1) the hadith of Abu Sa'id al-Khudri prohibiting women from traveling three days without a mahram, which indicates that three days is the threshold for travel; (2) the hadith on wiping over khuffayn, where the Prophet specified three days for a traveler; and (3) the practice of Ibn Umar and Ibn Abbas shortening prayer and breaking fast at a distance of four barid (16 farsakh = 48 miles). The text then discusses the disagreement with Imam Shafi'i, who holds that a journey of one day and one night suffices for qasr, based on the hadith prohibiting a woman from traveling one day without a mahram. The Hanafi response is that the three-day hadith is more specific and that caution dictates the longer distance in light of multiple evidences. Finally, the text explains the traditional measurements: barid, farsakh, and mile, citing Radd al-Muhtar to confirm that one farsakh = 3 miles and one shar'i mile = 4000 cubits. The Hanafi position is the relied-upon (mu'tamad) view in the school.

Key Terms
سفر (safar): Journey that changes certain rulings (e.g., qasr, fidyah, wiping over khuffayn for 3 days).
مسيرة (masirah): The distance or journey time required to traverse a route.
برد (barid): A unit of distance used in the Arab world, equal to four farsakh (12 Hanafi miles).
فرسخ (farsakh): A unit of distance equal to three Hanafi miles (approx. 6 km).
ميل شرعي (mil shari'i): A legal mile equal to 4000 cubits (approx. 2 km).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi madhhab, what is the minimum distance of travel that allows a person to shorten (qasr) their prayer?
A) One day and one night
B) Three days journey by camel or foot (approx. 48 Hanafi miles)
C) Two days journey
D) Any distance as long as the intention is to travel

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school defines travel for qasr as a journey of three days (48 Hanafi miles/55.5 English miles) based on specific hadith and ihtiyat, differing from the Shafi'i one-day threshold.

Defining Shar'i Travel Distance for Qasr and Iftaar
Defining shar'i travel distance for qasr and iftaar with Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of shar'i travel distance, derive it from the practice of the Companions, and distinguish it from the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
Qasr requires three days' travel (48 shar'i miles) according to the Hanafi school, based on the practice of Ibn Umar and Ibn Abbas.

This lesson examines the Hanafi school's definition of the minimum travel distance required for the concessions of qasr (shortening prayer) and iftaar (breaking fast in Ramadan). The passage presents the Hanafi position, supported by the practice of the Companions, and contrasts it with the Shafi'i view based on a hadith. Understanding this dispute is essential for applying the correct ruling in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its condition
The concession of qasr is conditional upon a valid travel (safar shar'i). Determine what constitutes that travel according to the Hanafi school.
2
State the Hanafi position
The Hanafi school holds that travel must be a journey of three days (by moderate pace on camel or foot), equated to 48 shar'i miles (approx. 87.8 km).
3
Locate the Hanafi proof
The proof is the practice of Ibn Umar and Ibn Abbas, who performed qasr and iftaar at a distance of four barid (16 farsakh = 48 shar'i miles). This athar is found in Sahih al-Bukhari.
4
Identify the opposing view
Imam al-Shafi'i considers a journey of one day and one night sufficient for qasr, based on a hadith where the Prophet (ﷺ) called such a distance 'safar' and prohibited women from traveling it without a mahram.
5
Analyze the Hanafi response
Imam Abu Hanifa took the cautious approach, relying on the explicit practice of the Companions over the general hadith, which he considered to be about prohibition of travel, not definition of travel for qasr.

كتاب الصلوة
مسيرة مسيرة ثلثة ايام بسير الابل و مشى الاقدام ولا معتبر فى ذلك بالسير في الماء. معلوم ہوتا کہ سفر کی مدت تین دن ہونی چاہئے۔ اسی کو سفر شرعی کہیں گے (۳) اس اثر سے بھی اس کی تائید ہوتی ہے کان ابن عمر وابن عباس يـقــصــران ويفطران في اربعة برد وهو ستة عشر فرسخا (الف) ( بخاری شریف، باب في كم يقصر الصلوۃ ص ۱۴۷ نمبر ۱۰۸۶) ایک فرسخ تین میل شرعی کا ہوتا ہے اس اعتبار سے سولہ فرسخ اڑتالیس میل ہوئے۔ اور انگریزی میل چھوٹا ہوتا ہے اس لئے وہ ساڑھے چون میل انگریزی ہوئے۔ ایک دن میں وسط چال کے ساتھ عمو ما سولہ میل سفر طے کر پاتے ہیں۔ اس لئے تین دن میں اڑتالیس میل ہوئے اور اصل تین دن کا سفر ہے۔ میل کو متین کرنا سہولت کے لئے ہے۔ نا امام شافعی کے نزدیک ایک دن ایک رات کی مسافت ہو تب بھی قصر کر سکتا ہے۔ ان کی دلیل یہ قول ہے سمى النبى الله السفر يوما و ليلة سفرا وفيه عن ابي هريرة قال قال النبي الا الله لا يحل لامرأة تؤمن بالله واليوم الآخر ان تسافر يوم وليلة ليس معها حرمة (ب) ( بخاری شریف، باب فی کم تقصیر الصلو ص ۳۸ نمبر ۱۰۸۸) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ ایک دن اور ایک رات کے سفر کو بھی سفر کہتے ہیں۔ اس لئے اس پر بھی قصر ہو سکتا ہے۔ امام ابو حنیفہ دلائل کی روشنی میں احتیاط کی طرف گئے ہیں۔
مقصد : جانے کی جگہ، قصد کرنے کی جگہ، مسیر : سیر سے مشتق ہے، سفر۔
فرسخ میل اور کیلو میٹر کا حساب ہے
پچھلے زمانے میں عرب میں بردہ فریح اور غلوہ رائج تھے، بعد میں میں شرعی آیا اور ابھی دنیا میں انگریزی میل اور کیلو میٹر کا حساب رائج ہے۔ اس لئے ان کی تفصیل یہ ہے۔
ایک بردچار فرسخ کا ہوتا ہے۔ اور ایک فرسخ تین شرعی میل کا ہوتا۔ اور ایک شرعی میل چار ہزار ہاتھ یعنی دو ہزار گز کا ہوتا ہے۔ اس طرح ایک برد بارہ شرعی میل کا ہوا۔ ایک برد چار فریخ کا ہوتا ہے اس کا ذکر عبد اللہ بن عباس کے اثر میں گزرا۔ کان ابن عمر وابن عباس يقصران و يفطر ان فی اربعه بر دو ھو ستة عشر فرسخا ( بخاری شریف، باب في كم يقصر الصلوۃ ص ۱۴۷، نمبر ۱۰۸۶) اس اثر میں ہے کہ چار بر دسولہ فرسخ کا ہوتا تھا۔ یعنی ایک برد چار فریخ کا ۔ اور چار بر دسولہ فرسخ کا ہوا جس پر عبد اللہ بن عمر اور عبد اللہ بن عباس سفر کا حکم لگاتے تھے۔ اور ایک فرسخ تین میل کا ہوتا ہے۔ اور ایک میل شرعی چار ہزار ہاتھ کا۔ جس کا مطلب یہ ہوا کہ دو ہزار گز کا میل ہوا۔ اس کی دلیل در مختار کی یہ عبارت ہے۔ الفریخ : ثلاثۃ امیال والمیل : اربعة آلاف ذراع ( ردالمت) علی در المختار، باب صلوۃ المسافر ، ج ثانی ،ص ۷۲۵ ) اس عبارت سے معلوم ہوا کہ فرسخ تین میل کا ہوتا ہے۔ اور ایک میل شرعی دو ہزار گز کا ہوتا ہے۔ اب بارہ فرسخ کوتین میل سے ضرب دیں تو 48 میل شرعی ہوئے ۔
حاشیہ : (الف) حضرت ابن عمر اور حضرت ابن عباس نماز قصر کرتے اور روزے کا افطار کرتے چار برد کے سفر میں جو سولہ فرسخ ہوتے (ب ) حضور نے سفر ایک دن ایک رات کو قرار دیا ہے۔ چنانچہ آپ نے فرمایا کسی عورت کے لئے حلال نہیں ہے جو اللہ اور آخرت کے دن پر ایمان رکھتی ہو کہ ایک دن اور ایک رات کی مسافت پر سفر کرے کہ اس کے ساتھ محرم نہ ہو۔

میل شرعی
میل انگریزی
کیلو میٹر
Book of Prayer. Journey distance: three days' travel by camel and on foot, and no consideration is given to water travel for that. It is known that the duration of travel must be three days. That is called shar'i travel. (3) This is supported by the athar: 'Ibn Umar and Ibn Abbas used to shorten prayer and break fast in a distance of four barid, which is sixteen farsakh' (a) (Bukhari, Chapter on how much to shorten prayer, p. 147, no. 1086). One farsakh is three shar'i miles. Hence sixteen farsakh equals forty-eight miles. And the English mile is smaller, so it becomes fifty-four and a half English miles. In one day, with moderate pace, one usually covers about sixteen miles. Therefore, three days make forty-eight miles, and the actual journey is three days. Defining the mile is for convenience. According to Imam al-Shafi'i, if the distance is one day and one night, one may still shorten prayer. His proof is the saying: 'The Prophet (ﷺ) called a journey of one day and one night a journey.' And from Abu Hurairah: 'The Prophet (ﷺ) said: It is not lawful for a woman who believes in Allah and the Last Day to travel a distance of one day and one night without a mahram.' (b) (Bukhari, Chapter on how much to shorten prayer, p. 38, no. 1088). From this hadith it is understood that a journey of one day and one night is also called a journey. Therefore, shortening prayer may apply to it. Imam Abu Hanifa, in light of evidences, went towards caution.

Maqsad: place of going, place of intending; masir: derived from sayr, travel.

Calculation of farsakh, mile, and kilometer
In earlier times, barid, farsakh, and ghalua were prevalent among the Arabs; later, the shar'i mile came, and now in the world, English mile and kilometer calculation are prevalent. So their details are as follows:
One barid equals four farsakh. And one farsakh equals three shar'i miles. And one shar'i mile equals four thousand cubits, i.e., two thousand yards. Thus one barid equals twelve shar'i miles. One barid equals four farsakh, as mentioned in the athar of Abdullah ibn Abbas: 'Ibn Umar and Ibn Abbas used to shorten prayer and break fast in four barid, which is sixteen farsakh' (Bukhari, Chapter on how much to shorten prayer, p. 147, no. 1086). In this athar, four barid was sixteen farsakh, i.e., one barid equals four farsakh. And four barid equals sixteen farsakh, on which Abdullah ibn Umar and Abdullah ibn Abbas applied the ruling of travel. And one farsakh equals three miles. And one shar'i mile equals four thousand cubits, meaning two thousand yards. The proof is the text from Durr al-Mukhtar: 'Al-Farsakh: three miles, and the mile: four thousand cubits' (Radd al-Muhtar 'ala al-Durr al-Mukhtar, Bab Salat al-Musafir, vol. 2, p. 725). From this text, it is known that a farsakh is three miles, and one shar'i mile is two thousand yards. Now multiply twelve farsakh by three miles, it becomes 48 shar'i miles.

Footnote: (a) Hadrat Ibn Umar and Hadrat Ibn Abbas used to shorten prayer and break fast in a journey of four barid, which is sixteen farsakh. (b) The Prophet (ﷺ) designated a journey as one day and one night. Thus he said: 'It is not lawful for a woman who believes in Allah and the Last Day to travel a distance of one day and one night without a mahram.'

(Difference between shar'i mile, English mile, and kilometer)
The English mile is 1.1363 times smaller than the shar'i mile, because the English mile is 1760 yards. And the kilometer is 1.828 times smaller than the shar'i mile, because the kilometer is only 1093.69 yards. And the kilometer is 1.6092 percent smaller than the English mile. By this calculation, 48 shar'i miles equal 54.545 English miles and 87.782 kilometers, on which the ruling of shortening prayer can be applied. Calculate with a calculator.

(Barid, farsakh, kilometer, and mile calculation at a glance)
How much smaller?
Which one?
One shar'i mile: 2000 yards
One English mile: 1760 yards - 1.1363
One kilometer: 1093.69 yards - 1.8288

One farsakh: 3 shar'i miles
One farsakh: 3.409 English miles
One farsakh: 5.486 kilometers
One barid: 4 farsakh
One barid: 12 shar'i miles
One barid: 13.635 English miles
One barid: 21.936 kilometers
16 farsakh: 48 shar'i miles
16 farsakh: 54.545 English miles
16 farsakh: 87.782 kilometers
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the legal definition of shar'i travel distance (masafat al-safar) for the application of qasr (shortening of prayer) and iftaar (breaking fast in Ramadan). The author begins by stating the Hanafi position: the travel distance must be three days' journey by camel or on foot, not by water. This is based on the practice of the Companions, as reported in the athar of Ibn Umar and Ibn Abbas, who performed qasr and iftaar when traveling a distance of four barid, which is sixteen farsakh. The author then explains the conversion: one farsakh equals three shar'i miles, so sixteen farsakh equals forty-eight shar'i miles. Since one day of moderate travel covers about sixteen miles, three days total forty-eight miles, confirming the three-day standard. The author mentions that this is the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school, as Imam Abu Hanifa took the more cautious approach in light of the evidences.

The passage then presents the opposing view of Imam al-Shafi'i, who considers a journey of one day and one night sufficient for qasr. His proof is the hadith in which the Prophet (ﷺ) called a journey of one day and one night a 'safar' and forbade a woman from traveling such a distance without a mahram. The Shafi'i argument is that since the shari'ah itself uses the term 'safar' for that distance, the concession of qasr should apply to it. The Hanafi response, though not explicitly elaborated here, is that the three-day distance is more consistently backed by the practice of the Companions and is the stricter view, hence adopted by Imam Abu Hanifa. The thamara al-khilaf (practical difference) is that under the Shafi'i view, a traveler may shorten prayer after covering approximately 54.5 English miles (one day), while under the Hanafi view, he must wait until approximately 87.8 kilometers (three days). The remainder of the passage provides precise conversions between shar'i miles, English miles, and kilometers, facilitating practical application. The author includes a table summarizing the relationships between barid, farsakh, shar'i mile, English mile, and kilometer. This detailed calculation is not a separate legal ruling but a scholastic aid to determine the actual distance required for the Hanafi ruling.

Key Terms
سفر شرعی (safar shar'i): Journey that meets the legal distance requirement for concessions like qasr and iftaar; defined as three days' travel (48 shar'i miles) in the Hanafi school.
قصر (qasr): Shortening the four-rak'ah obligatory prayers to two rak'ahs during travel.
میل شرعی (mil shar'i): A unit of distance equal to 4000 cubits (2000 yards), used in classical Hanafi fiqh to measure travel distance.
فرسخ (farsakh): A unit of distance equal to 3 shar'i miles (approx. 5.486 km).
برد (barid): A unit of distance equal to 4 farsakh, or 12 shar'i miles (approx. 21.936 km).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) One day and one night (approx. 54.5 English miles)
B) Three days' journey (48 shar'i miles, approx. 87.8 km)
C) Any distance beyond city limits
D) Two days' journey

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school defines shar'i travel as three days' journey (48 shar'i miles), based on the practice of Ibn Umar and Ibn Abbas, distinguishing it from the Shafi'i view of one day and night.

Traveler Praying Four Rak'ahs After Sitting for Tashahhud
Ruling on a traveler who prays four rak'ahs and sat for tashahhud in the second

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a traveler prays four rak'ahs after sitting for tashahhud in the second, and explain why the first two count as fard and the last two as nafl.

Core Idea
A traveler's first two rak'ahs fulfill the fard; extra rak'ahs become nafl, making the prayer valid but disliked.

The passage addresses a common scenario for a traveler (musafir) who intends qasr (shortened prayer) but ends up praying four rak'ahs. The key condition for validity is sitting after the second rak'ah for the duration of tashahhud. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its basis, and why it is considered disliked but acceptable.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and the Condition
The act is a traveler praying four rak'ahs when qasr (two) was intended. The condition specified is that he sat after the second rak'ah for the time of tashahhud.
The sitting after two is crucial; without it, the ruling differs.
2
Determine the Ruling for the First Two Rak'ahs
The text states 'the two rak'ahs suffice him for his obligation.' So the first two count as the fard (obligatory) prayer.
Suffice here means the obligation is discharged.
3
Classify the Additional Two Rak'ahs
The remaining two rak'ahs are considered nafl (supererogatory). They do not invalidate the fard but are extra.
This is because the fard was completed with the sitting.
4
Understand the Overall Validity and Dislike
The prayer is valid (sahih), but it is makruh tanzihan (slightly disliked) because the traveler delayed the salam by adding nafl without necessity.
The dislike is not prohibitive; the prayer is accepted.
5
Note the Necessity of the Sitting
If the traveler had not sat after the second rak'ah, the whole prayer could become invalid or entirely nafl, depending on intention. The sitting demarcates the fard portion.
This condition is key to the validity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فان صلى اربعا وقد قعد فى الثانية مقدار التشهد اجزأته الركعتان عن فرضه وكانت الاخريان له نافلة
If he prayed four [rak'ahs] and he sat in the second [rak'ah] the amount of tashahhud, the two rak'ahs suffice him for his obligation, and the other two are supererogatory for him.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage states a ruling: when a traveler (musafir) intends to pray two rak'ahs (qasr) but mistakenly or deliberately prays four rak'ahs, if he sits for tashahhud after the second rak'ah, his prayer is valid. The first two rak'ahs count as his fard (obligatory prayer), and the additional two become nafl (supererogatory). The Hanafi position is that this is valid but makruh (disliked) because he delayed the fard salam by adding nafl. The ruling is based on the principle that once the obligation is fulfilled by the first two rak'ahs (with the sitting), the remaining rak'ahs are voluntary. The Urdu commentary clarifies that this involves combining nafl with fard, which is disliked but valid.

Key Terms
اجزأته (ajza'athu): The two rak'ahs suffice for his obligation; the fard is fulfilled.
قعد فى الثانية مقدار التشهد (qa'ada fi al-thaniyah miqdar al-tashahhud): He sat after the second rak'ah for the duration of tashahhud (the prescribed time to recite the greeting).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A traveler who prays four rak'ahs after sitting for tashahhud in the second has his first two rak'ahs count as fard and the last two as nafl; the prayer is valid but disliked.

Traveler's Four Rak'ahs Without Tashahhud Sitting
Ruling on traveler's prayer with omitted sitting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a traveler omits the sitting for tashahhud in the second rak'ah while praying four rak'ahs, and explain why the prayer becomes batil.

Core Idea
Omitting the wajib sitting after two rak'ahs in a traveler's four-rak'ah prayer invalidates the entire prayer.

This lesson addresses a specific scenario: a traveler (musafir) who intends to pray a four-rak'ah prayer but does not sit for tashahhud after the second rak'ah. According to the Hanafi school, this omission renders the prayer batil (invalid). Understanding this ruling requires knowing the distinction between rukn (pillar) and wajib (obligatory element) in prayer, particularly the status of the final sitting (qadah akhirah).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and intended ruling
The act is a traveler praying four rak'ahs. Normally, a traveler shortens (qasr) four-rak'ah prayers to two. If he prays four, the first two become the fard, and the last two are nafl. The ruling in the text concerns the omission of the sitting after the second rak'ah.
The text assumes the traveler prays four without qasr.
2
Determine the missing element
The missing element is the sitting (qadah) after the second rak'ah for the amount of tashahhud. In Hanafi fiqh, this sitting is wajib (obligatory) for completing the prayer structure.
It is not a rukn (pillar) but a wajib; however, omitting a wajib without compensation can invalidate the prayer.
3
Understand the consequence: batil
Because the sitting was omitted, the first two rak'ahs (the fard portion) are deemed incomplete. The entire prayer becomes batil (invalid) and must be repeated.
Batil means the act has no legal validity and carries no reward; it must be repeated.
4
Apply the Hanafi classification
In Hanafi usul, the omission of a wajib element (like the sitting in the second rak'ah) renders the prayer batil unless compensated by sajdah al-sahw. However, this specific omission cannot be compensated because it disrupts the sequence of the fard.
This is distinct from missing a sunnah, which does not invalidate the prayer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان لم يقعد فى الثانية مقدار التشهد في الركعتين الأوليين بطلت صلوته
And if he did not sit in the second [rak'ah] the amount of tashahhud in the first two rak'ahs, his prayer becomes invalid.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage presents the opposite ruling: if a traveler (musafir) prays four rak'ahs but does not sit for tashahhud after the second rak'ah, his prayer is invalid (batil). The reason is that the first two rak'ahs were intended to be the fard, but the final sitting (qadah akhirah) is obligatory (wajib) for completing the prayer. By omitting it and continuing to the third rak'ah, he has disturbed the structure of the fard prayer. The Urdu commentary adds that if he omitted the sitting, the first two rak'ahs become nafl and the whole prayer is considered invalid due to the defect in the fard. The Hanafi ruling is that such a prayer must be repeated.

Key Terms
بطلت صلوته (batalat salatuhu): His prayer becomes invalid (batil), meaning it is not considered a valid Islamic prayer and must be repeated.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) Because he intended to pray qasr but prayed four.
D) Because he did not recite tashahhud after the first rak'ah.

Key Takeaway
Omitting the wajib sitting after the second rak'ah in a traveler's four-rak'ah prayer makes the entire prayer batil and must be repeated.

The 15-Day Threshold: When a Traveler Becomes a Resident
Ruling on intention of 15 days to become muqim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the minimum intention for a traveler to become a muqim and apply it to different scenarios of intended stay.

Core Idea
Fifteen days' intention transforms a traveler into a resident for prayer rulings.

This passage addresses a critical question for the traveling Muslim: when does one cease to be a musafir and become a muqim? The Hanafi school sets a clear threshold: intention to stay fifteen days or more makes completing prayers (four rak'ahs) wajib. Let's explore the evidence from the hadith of Ibn 'Abbas and the reasoning behind the chosen number.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its subject
The passage states that a traveler remains under the rulings of travel until he intends to stay in a town for fifteen days. That intention makes him a muqim for prayer, so he must pray complete (four rak'ahs). Identify that the subject is the traveler's prayer obligation.
2
Locate the evidence (dalil)
The hadith of Ibn 'Abbas (RA) is cited: the Prophet (SAW) stayed 19 days in Mecca shortening prayer. Hanafi jurists inferred that since 19 days allowed qasr, a longer stay would require completion. They set 15 days as a clear minimum below 19.
Hadith is zanni al-thubut and zanni al-dalala, so the ruling is wajib, not fard.
3
Understand the derivation (wajh al-dalalah)
The operative phrase is 'nineteen days shortening' – the Prophet shortened up to 19 days, implying that beyond that he would complete. The Hanafis adopted 15 days as a round number less than 19 to provide a definite criterion.
Other narrations (17, 18, 10 days) are reconciled by considering that the Prophet did not intend actual residency (watan al-iqamah).
4
Compare with other schools
The Shafi'i school uses the hadith of Anas (10 days) and the Prophet's stay in Mecca after conquest (4 days?) to set 4 days. The Hanafi response distinguishes between intended residency (15 days) and actual stay without intention.
The ikhtilaf is legitimate; both schools rely on authentic hadith but interpret differently.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يزال على حكم المسافر حتى ينوى الاقامة في بلدة خمسة عشر يوما
And he remains under the ruling of a traveler until he intends to stay in a town for fifteen days.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage determines when a traveler (musafir) ceases to be a traveler and becomes a resident (muqim) for prayer purposes. The Hanafi position, as presented here with the figure of fifteen days, is that if a traveler intends to stay in a town for fifteen days or more, he must perform complete prayers (four rak'ahs for Zuhr, Asr, and Isha). If he intends to stay less than fifteen days, he continues to shorten. The Urdu commentary explains that this is based on the practice of the Prophet (peace be upon him) during the Conquest of Mecca, as narrated by Ibn 'Abbas (19 days) and others. The different narrations (17, 18, 19, 10 days) are reconciled by understanding that the Prophet did not intend a fixed residence (watan al-‘iqamah), so the Hanafi scholars derived 15 days as the minimum to establish residency. The hadith from Anas (10 days in Mecca) is reconciled by considering that the Prophet did not intend to stay beyond 10 days, so qasr applied.

Key Terms
ينوى الاقامة في بلدة خمسة عشر يوما (yanwi al-iqamah fi baldatin khamsata 'ashara yawman): He intends to stay in a town for fifteen days. This is the minimum period that transforms a traveler into a muqim for prayer rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) 15 days
C) 19 days
D) 20 days

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school uses the intention to stay 15 days or more as the threshold that transforms a traveler into a resident (muqim) for the purpose of completing prayers.

The Duration of Stay That Obligates Complete Prayer: Hanafi & Shafi'i Views
Establishing the Hanafi ruling on when a traveler must complete the prayer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the minimum stay that obligates itmam, identify its proof from the Sunnah, and compare it with the Shafi'i four-day position.

Core Idea
A traveler must intend 15 days or more to complete prayer; anything less remains qasr.

This passage examines a key difference between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding the threshold for a traveler to pray full rak'ahs. While both agree that prolonged stay converts the traveler into a resident, they differ on the minimum duration. The Hanafi school, relying on the Prophet's practice and the Companions' fatwas, sets the boundary at fifteen days. Understanding this ruling is essential for any Muslim traveling for work, study, or leisure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi Ruling
Read the text and extract the exact condition that triggers itmam: intending to stay in a single town for 15 days or more.
The intention (niyyah) is key; the actual stay may differ.
2
Examine the Hanafi Dalil
Find the hadith of Ibn Abbas (Prophet stayed 15 days during Conquest) and the athar of Ibn Umar (resolved on 15 days). Understand how these prove the threshold.
The Hanafis interpret the Prophet's shortening during 15 days as indicating that less than 15 is not residence.
3
Contrast with the Shafi'i Position
Note that the Shafi'i school requires completion for an intended stay of 4 days. Their evidence comes from the Prophet's Hajj stay (10 days total but only 4 consecutive in Mecca) and the athar of Ibn al-Musayyab.
The Shafi'i view relies on the same narrations but different interpretation.
4
Understand the Thamarah al-Khilaf
Apply the difference to a specific case: a traveler intending 10 days. Hanafi shortens, Shafi'i completes. This shows the practical impact.
This difference matters for daily practice, especially in regions with mixed madhhab populations.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi Tarjih
The text presents the 15-day rule as the middle path between conflicting narrations (19, 18, 17, 10 days). Reflect on why the Hanafis consider this the strongest reconciliation.
The Hanafi approach prioritizes consistency and compromise among proofs.

هد الله
حدیث میں ہے عن ابن عباس قال اقام النبی الله تسعة عشر يقصر فنحن اذا سافرنا تسعة عشر قصرنا وان زدنا اتممنا (ج) ( بخاری شریف، باب ما جاء فی التقصیر کم باقیم حتی یقصر ص ۱۴۷ نمبر ۱۰۸۰ را ابو داؤد شریف ، باب متى يتم المسافرص ۱۸۰ نمبر ۱۲۲۹) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوتا ہے کہ آپ مکہ مکرمہ میں انہیں روز رہے ہیں۔ ابودا د کی حدیث سے معلوم ہوتا ہے کہ اٹھارہ روز رہے ہیں ۔ اور ایک روایت سے معلوم ہوتا ہے کہ سترہ روز رہے ہیں اور پھر بھی قصر کرتے رہے ہیں۔ اس کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ سترہ دن یا انہیں دن سے کم اقامت کرے تو قصر کرے گا۔ اور بخاری شریف ، مسلم شریف اور ابو داؤد شریف کی دوسری حدیث سے معلوم ہوتا ہے کہ آپ مکہ میں دس دن ٹھہرے تھے۔ سمعت انسا يقول خرجنا مع النبي الا الله من المدينة الى مكة فكان يصلی رکعتین رکعتین حتی رجعنا الى المدينة قلت اقمتم بمكة شيئا؟ قال اقمنا عشرا (د) ( بخاری شریف، باب ما جاء فی التقصير وكم يقيم حتى يقصر ص ۱۴۷ نمبر ۱۰۸۱
حاشیه : (الف) انس بن مالک فرماتے ہیں کہ میں نے حضور کے ساتھ ظہر کی نماز مدینہ میں چار رکعت پڑھی اور عصر کی نماز ذوالحلیفہ میں دو رکعت پڑھی (ب) حضرت علی جب بصرہ کی طرف نکلے تو بانس کا گھر دیکھا تو فرمایا اگر یہ بانس کا گھر نہ ہوتا تو میں دو رکعت نماز پڑھتا۔ میں نے کہا خص کیا چیز ہے؟ کیا بانس کا گھر (ج) ابن عباس فرماتے ہیں کہ آپ انہیں دن ٹھہرے اور قصر کرتے رہے۔ پس اگر ہم سفر کریں اور انہیں دن ٹھہریں تو قصر کریں اور اگر زیادہ کریں تو اتمام کریں (د) حضرت انسؓ نے فرمایا کہ ہم حضور کے ساتھ مدینہ سے مکہ کے لئے نکلے تو آپ دو دورکعت نماز پڑھتے رہے۔ یہاں تک کہ مدینہ واپس آئے۔ میں نے کہا کہ مکہ میں

كتاب الصلوة
عشر يوما فصاعدا فيلزمه الاتمام فان نوى الاقامة اقل من ذلك لم يتم [۳۳۴](۷) و من دخل ولم ينو ان يقيم فيه خمسة عشر يوما وانما يقول غدا اخرج او بعد غد اخرج حتى ر مسلم شریف فصل الى منى قصر اذا اقام ببلد و ص ۲۴۳ نمبر ۶۹۳ را بو داؤد شریف، باب متی یتیم المسافرص ۱۸۰ نمبر ۱۲۳۳) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ دس دن سے زائد کی اقامت کی نیت کرے گا تو ا تمام کرے گا۔ لیکن حنفیہ نے دونوں کے درمیان کو لیا ہے جو اوسط ہے۔ یعنی پندرہ دن کی اقامت کی نیت کرے گا تو ا تمام کرے گا۔ اور ان کی دلیل یہ حدیث ہے۔ (۱) عن ابن عباس قال اقام رسول الله بمكة عام الفتح خمس عشرة يقصر الصلوة (الف) (ابوداؤد شریف، باب متی یتیم المسافرص ۱۸۰ نمبر ۱۲۳۱ار ترمذی شریف، باب ماجاء فی کم تقتصر الصلوة ص ۱۲۲ نمبر ۵۴۸) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ پندرہ دن تک ٹھہرنے کی نیت کرے تو اتمام کرے گا (۲) اس کی تائید میں یہ اثر بھی ہے قال كان ابن عمر اذا اجمع على اقامة خمس عشرة سرح ظهره وصلى اربعا (ب) مصنف ابن ابی شیبۃ ۷۴۱ باب من قال اذا اجمع علی اقامتہ خمسة عشرۃ اتم ج ثانی ص ۲۱۱ نمبر ۱۸۲۱۷ مصنف بن عبد الرزاق ، باب الرجل يخرج في وقت الصلوة ج ثانی ص ۵۳۴ نمبر ۴۳۴۸) اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ وطن اقامت بنے میں پندرہ دن کا اعتبار ہے۔
قائد و امام شافعی کے نزدیک اگر چار دن مظہر نے کا ارادہ کرے تو اتمام کرے گا۔ او پر حد بث گزری کہ حضور حج کے موقع پر مکہ میں دس دن ٹھہرے تھے لیکن ایک ساتھ صرف مکہ میں تین دن ٹھہرے ہیں۔ پانچواں، چھنا اور ساتواں ذی الحجہ کو اور باقی دن منی ، مزدلفہ اور عرفات میں ٹھہرے ہیں۔ جس سے نتیجہ نکلتا ہے کہ اگر ایک ساتھ مکہ میں چار دن ٹھہرے تو اتمام کرے (۲) ان کی دلیل یہ اثر ہے عن ابن المسیب قال اذا اقمت بارض اربعا فصل اربعا (ج ) ( ترمذی شریف، باب ماجاء فی كم تقصیر الصلوۃ ص ۱۲۲ نمبر ۵۴۸ر مصنف عبدالرزاق، باب الرجل يخرج في وقت الصلوۃ ج ثانی ص ۵۳۴ نمبر ۴۳۴۶) اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ چار دن ٹھہرنے کی نیت کرے تو چار رکعت نماز پڑھے گا۔
Chapter of Prayer. The houses of the town. (6) And he remains on the ruling of the traveler until he intends to stay in a town for fifteen days or more, then completion becomes necessary for him. If he intends to stay less than that, he does not complete. A hadith: Anas ibn Malik said: I prayed Zuhr with the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) in Medina four rak'ahs, and Asr at Dhu al-Hulayfah two rak'ahs. (Bukhari) ... Explanation: He prayed four rak'ahs as long as he was in Medina, and when he left the town to Dhu al-Hulayfah, since he left the town, he prayed two rak'ahs. (2) An athar: Ali when he went out to Basra saw a hut and said: Were it not for this hut, I would have prayed two rak'ahs. I asked: What is a hut? He said: A house made of reeds. (Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq) Meaning: Outside Kufa there were bamboo huts, so Ali said that had they passed beyond those huts, they would have prayed two rak'ahs, but since they are near these huts, they pray four rak'ahs because they are still within the annexed area of the town. (6) He will always remain under the ruling of travel until he intends to stay in a town for fifteen days or more. Then completion becomes obligatory. If he intends less, he does not complete. If he intends to stay fifteen days in one town, that becomes a residence domain, so he prays four rak'ahs instead of two. If he intends less than fifteen, he shortens and does not complete because according to the Hanafis, less than fifteen days is not a residence domain. Hadith: Ibn Abbas said: The Prophet stayed nineteen (days) and shortened, so we also if we travel and stay nineteen we shorten, and if we exceed we complete. (Bukhari, Abu Dawud) This indicates that he stayed nineteen days in Mecca. Abu Dawud's hadith says eighteen days, another narration says seventeen, yet he shortened. The meaning is that if one stays seventeen or less, he shortens. Another hadith from Anas says we stayed ten days in Mecca. (Bukhari, Muslim, Abu Dawud) Footnotes: (a) Anas said: I prayed Zuhr with the Prophet in Medina four rak'ahs and Asr at Dhu al-Hulayfah two rak'ahs. (b) Ali saw a bamboo house and said if not for it I would pray two rak'ahs. (c) Ibn Abbas said the Prophet stayed nineteen days and shortened; so if we stay nineteen we shorten, if more we complete. (d) Anas said we stayed ten days. (Page 232) He intends ten days or more, then completion is necessary. If he intends less, he does not complete. (7) Whoever enters and does not intend to stay fifteen days, but keeps saying 'I will go out tomorrow' or 'the day after', etc. (Muslim, Abu Dawud) This hadith shows that if one intends to stay more than ten days, he completes. But the Hanafis took the middle path, i.e., fifteen days. Their proof is the hadith of Ibn Abbas: The Prophet stayed in Mecca during the Conquest for fifteen days, shortening the prayer. (Abu Dawud, Tirmidhi) This shows that if one intends to stay fifteen days, he completes. Supporting this is the athar of Ibn Umar: When he resolved to stay fifteen days, he would set forth and pray four rak'ahs. (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah, Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq) This shows that fifteen days are considered for residence domain. Imam al-Shafi'i holds that if one intends four days, he completes. He argues that the Prophet stayed in Mecca during Hajj ten days, but only three consecutive days in Mecca (5th, 6th, 7th of Dhul-Hijjah), and the rest in Mina, Muzdalifah, and Arafat, leading to the conclusion that staying four consecutive days requires completion. His proof is the athar of Ibn al-Musayyab: If you stay in a land for four days, pray four rak'ahs. (Tirmidhi, Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq) This indicates that if one intends four days, he prays four rak'ahs.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute concerning the minimum duration of stay that obligates a traveler to complete the prayer (itmam) instead of shortening (qasr). The author begins by stating the core Hanafi ruling: a traveler remains on the ruling of travel until he intends to stay in a single town for fifteen days or more; only then does the full prayer become obligatory. If he intends less than fifteen days, he continues to shorten. The evidence is provided through hadith and athar. The hadith of Anas establishes that the Prophet prayed four rak'ahs in Medina (his home) but shortened when he left the town boundaries. The athar of Ali illustrates that once one leaves the built-up area and its immediate surroundings (fina'), he is considered a traveler. The commentary then clarifies that the key is the intention (niyyah) of staying fifteen days, as this transforms the place into a 'watan iqamah' (residence domain). The Hanafis rely on the hadith of Ibn Abbas that the Prophet stayed in Mecca for fifteen days during the Conquest and shortened, implying that fifteen days is the threshold for completion. The athar of Ibn Umar supports this: he would complete prayer when he resolved to stay fifteen days. The passage then contrasts the Shafi'i position, which sets the threshold at four days based on the Prophet's stay during Hajj (ten days total but only four consecutive in Mecca) and the athar of Ibn al-Musayyab. The Hanafi response is implied by maintaining the fifteen-day rule as the middle path. The practical difference: a traveler intending exactly ten days would shorten according to Hanafis but complete according to Shafi'is.

Key Terms
وطن اقامت (watan iqamah): A place where one intends to stay for 15 days or more, making it a temporary residence that obligates complete prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what minimum intended stay in a town obligates a traveler to pray full rak'ahs?
A) 4 days
B) 10 days
C) 15 days
D) 19 days

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school uniquely sets the condition for completing prayer at 15 days based on the Prophet's practice and Companion rulings, forming a balanced middle ground among conflicting narrations.

The Ruling on Indefinite Stay without Intention
Clarifying that a traveler remains shortening despite prolonged stay without firm intention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a traveler who stays indefinitely without firm intention of 15 days and identify the proof from the athar of Ibn Umar.

Core Idea
Without a firm intention to stay fifteen days, even years of stay do not make one a resident.

This passage addresses a common scenario: a traveler enters a city with no fixed departure date, constantly saying 'I will leave tomorrow,' and ends up staying for years. According to the Hanafi school, such a person remains a traveler (musafir) and must continue to shorten prayers (qasr) as long as he has not formed a firm intention (pukhta irada) to stay fifteen days. The proof is the statement of Ibn Umar, which explicitly conditions full prayer on a firm resolution to stay fifteen days.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is staying in a city without a firm intention to stay 15 days. The ruling: such a person remains a traveler and shortens prayers (two rak'ahs).
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the athar of Ibn Umar in Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah: 'When he resolves to stay fifteen days, he sets forth and prays four rak'ahs.'
The Arabic key phrase is 'idha ajma'a' (when he resolves).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The word 'ajma'a' indicates that full prayer is conditioned upon a firm intention. The absence of such intention leaves the traveler in qasr.
This is the essential legal link: the ruling depends on intention, not duration.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some schools (e.g., Shafi'i) consider a stay of 4 days or actual residence as triggering completion. Hanafis insist on the 15-day intention criterion.
This difference has practical consequences for long-term travelers.
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: a traveler staying for years without intention still shortens in the Hanafi school, while other schools may require full prayers after a few days.
Thus, the Hanafi ruling provides continuity for those who never decide to settle.

(۱) جب تک کہ کسی ایک شہر میں پندرہ دن ٹھہرنے کا پختہ ارادہ نہ کرے اس وقت تک قصر کرتا رہے گا۔ اگر یہ کہے کہ آج جاؤں گا یا کل جاؤں گا۔ اس طرح کرتے کرتے کئی سال ٹھہر گیا تب بھی وہ مسافر ہی ہے۔ قصر کرتا رہے گا (۲) اس کا ثبوت مسئلہ نمبر 4 کے اثر میں ہے قال ابن عمر اذا اجمع على اقامة خمس عشر سرح ظهره وصلى اربعا (و) (مصنب ابن ابی شیبۃ ص۳۴۳) اس میں کہا گیا ہے کہ پندرہ رہنے کا پختہ ارادہ کرے تو اتمام کرے گا۔ اور اگر پختہ ارادہ نہ کرے تو قصر کرے گا (۳) حضور فتح مکہ کے موقع پر مکہ تشریف لائے حاشیہ : (الف) ابن عباس فرماتے ہیں کہ حضور مکہ میں فتح مکہ کے دن پندرہ دن ٹھہرے اور نماز قصر کرتے رہے (ب ) حضرت ابن عمر کہا کرتے تھے جب کہ پندرہ دن کے ٹھہرنے کا پختہ ارادہ کرے اور اپنی پیٹھ کو ہلکا کردے تو چار رکعت نماز پڑھے (ج) ابن مسیب نے فرمایا اگر کسی زمین میں چار روز ٹھہرے تو چار چار رکعت نماز پڑھے ( د ) ابن عمر فرماتے تھے جب پندرہ دن ٹھہرنے کا پختہ ارادہ کرے اور پیٹھ ہلکی کرے تو چار رکعت نماز پڑھے۔
(7) If someone enters a town and does not intend to stay there for fifteen days, but only keeps saying 'I will go out tomorrow' or 'the day after', until he remains there for many years, he will pray two rak'ahs. (1) As long as he does not firmly intend to stay fifteen days in one town, he will continue to shorten. If he says 'I will go today' or 'tomorrow', and in this manner he stays for many years, he is still a traveler; he continues to shorten. (2) Its proof is in the athar of issue number 4: Ibn Umar said: When he resolves to stay fifteen days, he sets forth and prays four rak'ahs. (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah) It states that if he firmly intends to stay fifteen, he completes; if he does not firmly intend, he shortens. (3) The Prophet came to Mecca during the Conquest. Footnotes: (a) Ibn Abbas said the Prophet stayed in Mecca for fifteen days during the Conquest and shortened the prayer. (b) Ibn Umar used to say: When he firmly intends to stay fifteen days and makes his back light (i.e., settles), he prays four rak'ahs. (c) Ibn al-Musayyab said: If you stay in a land for four days, pray four rak'ahs. (d) Ibn Umar said: When he firmly intends to stay fifteen days and makes his back light, he prays four rak'ahs.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with proof. It addresses a practical scenario: a person enters a city without intending to stay fifteen days, constantly postponing departure, and ends up staying for years. According to the Hanafi school, such a person remains a traveler and must shorten his prayers (qasr) because he has never formed a firm intention (azm) to stay fifteen days. The proof is derived from the athar of Ibn Umar, which explicitly conditions the completion of prayer on a firm resolve (ijma') to stay fifteen days. Without this resolve, shortening continues. The passage also mentions the Prophet's stay in Mecca during the Conquest, where he stayed fifteen days but still shortened (showing that even a stay of fifteen days without the firm intention? Actually, the Prophet did have intention, but the Hanafis explain that the Prophet was not making it a residence domain; he shortened because he was still traveling? The text uses it to emphasize the importance of intention. The footnotes repeat the key proofs.

Key Terms
پختہ ارادہ (pukhta irada): A firm and decisive intention to stay for a specific duration (15 days). Without this, one remains a traveler.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A traveler enters a city and says 'I will leave tomorrow' but keeps postponing for two years. According to the Hanafi school, how should he pray?
A) Full prayers after 15 days
B) Full prayers after 4 days
C) Shortened prayers until firm intention to stay 15 days
D) Full prayers because of long stay

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school makes the ruling of travel depend on a firm intention to stay fifteen days, not on the actual duration of stay.

The Hanafi 15-Day Rule for Traveller's Residence
General rule and inter-madhhab dispute on days of residence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position that intending 15 days obligates completion of prayer (itmam) and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the provided proofs.

Core Idea
Intending 15 days in one place makes a traveller a resident; less than that, qasr continues.

This lesson establishes the fundamental Hanafi rule for when a traveller must complete prayer. The text cites the Prophet's 15-day stay in Makkah during the Conquest and the practice of Ibn Umar. It also addresses the disagreement with Imam al-Shafi'i, who sets the threshold at four days. Understanding this ruling is essential for correctly applying the concessions of travel.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
State what act is being ruled upon and the hukm level. Here, the act is the traveller's prayer; the ruling is that wajib (itmam) applies when intending 15 days.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the proof cited: the hadith of Ibn Abbas that the Prophet stayed 15 days in Makkah and shortened. This is used by Hanafis to infer the threshold.
Hanafi reasoning: since the Prophet shortened, he had not firmly intended residence; thus 15 days without firm intention does not make one a resident. Conversely, if one intends 15 days, that firm intention creates residency.
3
Understand the wajh al-dalalah
Explain how the hadith proves the Hanafi position. The operative point is the length of stay combined with the absence of firm intention.
4
Compare with the opposing school
State the Shafi'i position (4 days) and its proof (athar of Ibn al-Musayyib). Then examine the Hanafi response: the Prophet's Hajj stay was not continuous in one place.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical difference: a traveller intending 4 days completes according to Shafi'i, but shortens according to Hanafi.
